                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Extreme haplotype variation in the desiccation-tolerant clubmoss Selaginella lepidophylla"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2017 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-01-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Plant genome size varies by four orders of magnitude, and most of this variation stems from dynamic changes in repetitive DNA content. Here we report the small 109&#8201;Mb genome of Selaginella lepidophylla, a clubmoss with extreme desiccation tolerance. Single-molecule sequencing enables accurate haplotype assembly of a single heterozygous S. lepidophylla plant, revealing extensive structural variation. We observe numerous haplotype-specific deletions consisting of largely repetitive and heavily methylated sequences, with enrichment in young Gypsy LTR retrotransposons. Such elements are active but rapidly deleted, suggesting &#8220;bloat and purge&#8221; to maintain a small genome size. Unlike all other land plant lineages, Selaginella has no evidence of a whole-genome duplication event in its evolutionary history, but instead shows unique tandem gene duplication patterns reflecting adaptation to extreme drying. Gene expression changes during desiccation in S. lepidophylla mirror patterns observed across angiosperm resurrection plants. 
                        Selaginella lepidophylla is a clubmoss with extreme desiccation tolerance. Here, the authors assemble its highly heterozygotic haplotypes and examine gene expression changes during desiccation, which shed light on the mechanisms for maintaining a small genome size and adaptation to extreme drying."/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Plant genome size varies by four orders of magnitude, and most of this variation stems from dynamic changes in repetitive DNA content. Here we report the small 109 Mb genome of <i>Selaginella lepidophylla</i>, a clubmoss with extreme desiccation tolerance. Single-molecule sequencing enables accurate haplotype assembly of a single heterozygous <i>S. lepidophylla</i> plant, revealing extensive structural variation. We observe numerous haplotype-specific deletions consisting of largely repetitive and heavily methylated sequences, with enrichment in young <i>Gypsy</i> LTR retrotransposons. Such elements are active but rapidly deleted, suggesting “bloat and purge” to maintain a small genome size. Unlike all other land plant lineages, <i>Selaginella</i> has no evidence of a whole-genome duplication event in its evolutionary history, but instead shows unique tandem gene duplication patterns reflecting adaptation to extreme drying. Gene expression changes during desiccation in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> mirror patterns observed across angiosperm resurrection plants.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The ability to survive periodic drying was essential for the aquatic ancestors of terrestrial plants during their transition from sea to land. These early protective mechanisms are thought to be ancestral and conserved in most land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Oliver, M. J., Tuba, Z. &amp; Mishler, B. D. The evolution of vegetative desiccation tolerance in land plants. Plant Ecol. &#xA;                           151, 85–100 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d29428e527">1</a></sup>, forming the basis of modern seed and pollen desiccation pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Costa, M. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants &#xA;                           3, 17038 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d29428e531">2</a></sup>. A small group of plants have maintained this ancestral desiccation tolerance, surviving near complete anhydrobiosis (&lt;10% relative water content) during prolonged drought events. Desiccation tolerance is widespread in non-seed plants such as mosses, ferns, and lycophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Proctor, M. The physiological basis of bryophyte production. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. &#xA;                           104, 61–77 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d29428e535">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Lüttge, U., Beck, E. &amp; Bartels, D. Plant desiccation tolerance. Vol. 215 (Springer, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d29428e538">4</a></sup>, but is uncommon in angiosperms. High-quality genomes for several angiosperm resurrection plants have shed light on the molecular basis of desiccation tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Costa, M. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants &#xA;                           3, 17038 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d29428e542">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature &#xA;                           527, 508–511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d29428e545">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Xiao, L. et al. The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: A blueprint for survival of dehydration. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. &#xA;                           112, 5833–5837 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d29428e548">6</a></sup>, but the sparse genomic resources for non-seed plants has hindered progress toward understanding the origin of this trait.</p><p>Here we report the small genome of <i>Selaginella lepidophylla</i>, a lycophyte that can survive the loss of &gt;95% cellular water for decades in a quiescent state. <i>Selaginella</i> are among the earliest diverging lineages of vascular plants, and extant species bear striking morphological similarities to fossils from the upper Carboniferous (333–350 Myr ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Banks, J. A. Selaginella and 400 million years of separation. Annu. Rev. Plant. Biol. &#xA;                           60, 223–238 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d29428e561">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Thomas, B. A. Paleozoic herbaceous lycopsids and the beginnings of extant lycopodium sens lat. And Selaginella Sens. Lat. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. &#xA;                           79, 623–631 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d29428e564">8</a></sup>. Genome size variation within this ancient group is minimal and <i>Selagienlla</i> are the only clade of land plants with no shared whole-genome duplication event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baniaga, A. E., Arrigo, N. &amp; Barker, M. S. The small nuclear genomes of Selaginella are associated with a low rate of genome size evolution. Genome Biol. Evol. &#xA;                           8, 1516–1525 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d29428e571">9</a></sup>. Detailed comparative genomics across angiosperms and with the <i>S. moellendorffii</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. science &#xA;                           332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d29428e579">10</a></sup> shed light the origin of desiccation tolerance and unique properties of <i>Selaginella</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Haplotype-level genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Extensive within genome heterozygosity exists for most plant species, and this variation enhances fitness and serves as a reservoir for adaptive evolution. Heterozygosity is a major issue in plant genome assembly, and most heterozygous plant genomes are highly fragmented with collapsed haplotype regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. &#xA;                           24, 71–81 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d29428e598">11</a></sup>. <i>S. lepidophylla</i> has an estimated genome size of 109 Mb based on kmer frequency analyses, which is consistent with the small genomes observed across the genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baniaga, A. E., Arrigo, N. &amp; Barker, M. S. The small nuclear genomes of Selaginella are associated with a low rate of genome size evolution. Genome Biol. Evol. &#xA;                           8, 1516–1525 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d29428e605">9</a></sup>. Despite its compact size, the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome is highly heterozygous, with a bimodal distribution of kmers corresponding to distinct heterozygous and collapsed homozygous regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">1</a>). Initial sequencing attempts using an Illumina only approach yielded a highly collapsed and fragmented assembly of 21,282 scaffolds, totaling 54 Mb, with an N50 of 3 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">1</a>). To overcome assembly issues related to heterozygosity, we generated 175× coverage (19.3 Gb) of single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequences from PacBio with a filtered subread N50 length of 18 kb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">2</a>). The long read lengths of PacBio sequencing facilitate accurate separation and assembly of heterozygotic haplotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods &#xA;                           13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d29428e622">12</a></sup>. Raw PacBio reads were error corrected and assembled using the Canu assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d29428e626">13</a></sup>, which utilizes optimized overlapping and assembly algorithms to avoid collapsing repetitive regions and haplotypes. The resulting contigs were polished using the raw PacBio data with Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods &#xA;                           10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d29428e630">14</a></sup>, followed by a second round of polishing with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE &#xA;                           9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d29428e634">15</a></sup> using the high-coverage Illumina data.</p><p>The final <i>S. lepidophylla</i> assembly spans 122 Mb across 1149 contigs with an N50 length of 163 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">2</a>). These assembly metrics are similar to the <i>S. moellendorffii</i> genome (contig N50 length = 120 kb), which was sequenced using a reiterative Sanger approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. science &#xA;                           332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d29428e650">10</a></sup>. Our assembly includes 15 of the 20 terminal telomere tracks (2<i>n</i> = 20) and 128 rRNA arrays, which are typically unassembled in most next-generation sequencing (NGS) based genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. &#xA;                           24, 71–81 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d29428e658">11</a></sup>. The <i>S. lepidophylla</i> assembly graph is highly complex with numerous interconnected edges, reflecting bubbles and ambiguities caused by heterozygous regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig1">1</a>). The long PacBio reads assembled 930 haplotype blocks spanning 33.7 Mb of separated haplotype sequences (Methods section; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">3</a>). Other haplotype aware assemblers were tested but Canu provided the best separation of haplotype blocks as well as the best contiguity.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Genome assembly graph of <i>S. lepidophylla</i>. Each line (node) represents a contig with connections (edges) representing ambiguities in the graph structure. A subset of the graph showing heterozygous bubbles is enlarged. Contig color is randomly assigned</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                           <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Extensive haplotype-specific LTR retrotransposon accumulation and deletion. <b>a</b> Typical micro-collinearity between two <i>S. lepidophylla</i> haplotypes. <b>b</b> Estimated insertions times of intact <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> LTR retrotransposons. <b>c</b> Composition of haplotype specific and paired collinear regions. The proportion of genes and LTRs in the 105 manually curated insertions (left) and flanking collinear regions (right) are plotted. <b>d</b> Micro-collinearity between two haplotypes showing a large 57 kb deletion in contig 142. Green and blue bars delineate gene orientation. Predicted LTR elements are depicted in orange and the three complete Gypsy LTRs are denoted by arrows. The three complete Gypsy LTRs have insertion times of &lt;0.1 Ma</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We annotated 27,204 genes across a deduced 89 Mb haploid assembly, considerably more than <i>S. moellendorffii</i> (22,285 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. science &#xA;                           332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d29428e744">10</a></sup>. The majority (96%) of core eukaryotic BUSCOs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d29428e748">16</a></sup> were identified in the gene set, supporting the completeness of the genome assembly and quality of the annotation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">3</a>). Roughly 45% of genes (11,847) are found in duplicated pairs across the full 122 Mb assembly with an average protein homology of 99.1%. Gene density in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> is 4.0 kb per gene, which is higher than <i>S. moellendorffi</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. science &#xA;                           332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d29428e762">10</a></sup> and Arabidopsis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Initiative, A. G. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature &#xA;                           408, 796 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d29428e766">17</a></sup>, but lower than the smallest sequenced plant genome (82 Mb) of <i>Utricularia gibba</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Ibarra-Laclette, E. et al. Architecture and evolution of a minute plant genome. Nature &#xA;                           498, 94–98 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d29428e773">18</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome evolution and haplotype-specific structural variation</h3><p>Genome size varies by four orders of magnitude across land plants, ranging from 60 Mb in the carnivorous <i>Genlisea</i> to 152,000 Mb in <i>Paris japonica</i>. Lycophytes have a narrow variation in genome size compared to angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Leitch, I., Soltis, D., Soltis, P. &amp; Bennett, M. Evolution of DNA amounts across land plants (Embryophyta). Ann. Bot. &#xA;                           95, 207–217 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d29428e791">19</a></sup>, and <i>Selaginella</i> sizes range from 90 to 182 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baniaga, A. E., Arrigo, N. &amp; Barker, M. S. The small nuclear genomes of Selaginella are associated with a low rate of genome size evolution. Genome Biol. Evol. &#xA;                           8, 1516–1525 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d29428e798">9</a></sup>. Rapid changes in genome size are driven by bursts of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposon proliferation followed by aggressive deletion through unequal homologous recombination and double-strand break repair<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Michael, T. P. Plant genome size variation: bloating and purgingDNA. Brief. Funct. Genomics &#xA;                           13, 308–317 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d29428e803">20</a></sup>. LTR elements are distributed non-randomly across the genome with <i>Gypsy-</i>like elements predominantly found in gene poor and repeat rich blocks while <i>Copia</i>-like elements are dispersed among genes (Supplementary Table 4). Most of the LTR elements are clustered in a relatively small number of LTR families (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">4</a>). We identified 744 full-length LTR retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), including 572 <i>Gypsy</i> and 17 <i>Copia</i> elements (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">5</a>). The majority of intact LTRs are relatively young, with average insertion times of 0.1 and 0.4 MYA for <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> elements, respectively, and most intact LTR elements are younger than 0.5 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">5</a>). The proportion of young LTR elements is higher in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> than other high-quality plant genomes (Supplementary Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">5</a>). The recent LTR amplification suggests most elements have been active recently in <i>Selaginella</i> but they are rapidly fragmented and deleted. <i>S. lepidophylla</i> has a much higher ratio (4.06, Wilcoxon rank-sum, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05, same statistical test hereafter unless specified) of solo-intact LTR elements comparing to the genomes of rice and Arabidopsis (2.71 and 1.80, respectively) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">6</a>), indicating more effective removal of LTR-RTs via unequal homologous recombination. Moreover, the average age of LTR families in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> is much younger (0.37 MY vs. 0.92 and 0.94 MY, respectively; <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05) under the mutation rate of <i>μ</i> = 1.3 × 10–8 (per bp per year), suggesting rapid removal of LTR elements. The intensive and rapid removal of LTR-RTs is likely one of the causes of haplotype variation  and enrichment of LTR-RTs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig2">2</a>).</p><p>Haplotype blocks within the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome are largely collinear, with conserved gene content and 98.2% sequence homology (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">3</a>). However, since our analysis is based on both the number of contiguous protein and sequence homology, it is possible that we have missed large highly diverged haplotype blocks. A detailed comparison uncovered 104 large-scale haplotype-specific insertions or deletions (indels) across the genome. These high-confidence indels collectively span 2.5 Mb among 13.5 Mb of collinear sequences (18.5%) with individual sizes ranging from 4.8 kb to 85 kb with an average size of 24 kb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">6</a>). This is likely an underestimation as separate haplotypes were assembled for 35% of the genome and only high-confidence collinear regions spanning &gt;75 kb were surveyed. Haplotype-specific regions are largely repetitive with 58% of bases consisting of LTR retrotransposons and only 9% genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig2">2c</a>). Flanking collinear regions show the opposite trend, with 86% genic and only 11% LTR retrotransposon sequences. We annotated 335 protein-coding genes in the gene poor, haplotype-specific regions, but most (74%; 248 genes) have little to no detectable expression (TPM &lt;0.5). Young LTR retrotransposons (insertion times &lt;0.1 Ma) are over-represented in haplotype-specific regions, supporting a recent origin of these indels (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig2">2d</a>; <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01).</p><p>DNA methylation is associated with suppressing repetitive element proliferation and larger, repeat-dense plant genomes tend to have higher levels of methylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Niederhuth, C. E. et al. Widespread natural variation of DNA methylation within angiosperms. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           17, 194 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d29428e898">21</a></sup>. We surveyed genome-wide DNA methylation in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> for patterns related to repetitive element silencing. Similar to other non-seed vascular plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Zemach, A., McDaniel, I. E., Silva, P. &amp; Zilberman, D. Genome-wide evolutionary analysis of eukaryotic DNA methylation. Science &#xA;                           328, 916–919 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d29428e905">22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Takuno, S., Ran, J.-H. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Evolutionary patterns of genic DNA methylation vary across land plants. Nat. Plants &#xA;                           2, 15222 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d29428e908">23</a></sup>, <i>S. lepidophylla</i> lacks evidence of any gene body methylation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig3">3a</a>). In contrast, intact LTR retrotransposons have high levels of CpG and CHG methylation with comparatively little CHH methylation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig3">3b</a>). Methylation is highest in the body of LTR retrotransposons with levels dropping at the 5′- and 3′-flanking regions. Gene rich regions of the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome are highly compact with an average of 1.1 kb intergenic sequences with interspersed, repeat-dense blocks (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig3">3c</a>). This creates large tracks in the genome that are essentially devoid of methylation flanked by densely methylated, likely heterochromatic regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Global methylation patterns in the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome. <b>a</b> 
                                       <i>S. lepidophylla</i> lacks gene body methylation. Levels of CHH (green), CHG (blue) and CpG (red) methylation are plotted in the upstream (transcriptional start site; TSS), downstream (transcriptional termination site (TTS), and body of genes. <b>b</b> Intact LTR retrotransposons are highly methylated with decreasing levels at the 5′- and 3′-flanking regions. <b>c</b> Methylation levels are highly variable across the genome with strong correlation of LTR retrotransposon density. Top, genome landscape of genomic features across Contig 1. Bottom, heatmap of genomic features and methylation levels, where blue indicates low abundance and red signifies high abundance</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative genomics across <i>Selaginella</i> and angiosperms</h3><p>
                           <i>Selaginella</i> arose in the early Carboniferous period 333–350 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Thomas, B. A. Paleozoic herbaceous lycopsids and the beginnings of extant lycopodium sens lat. And Selaginella Sens. Lat. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. &#xA;                           79, 623–631 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d29428e977">8</a></sup>, and the ancestors of <i>S. lepidophylla</i> and <i>S. moellendorffii</i> diverged about 250 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Baniaga, A. E., Arrigo, N. &amp; Barker, M. S. The small nuclear genomes of Selaginella are associated with a low rate of genome size evolution. Genome Biol. Evol. &#xA;                           8, 1516–1525 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d29428e987">9</a></sup>, well before the radiance of modern angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Magallón, S., Gómez‐Acevedo, S., Sánchez‐Reyes, L. L. &amp; Hernández‐Hernández, T. A metacalibrated time‐tree documents the early rise of flowering plant phylogenetic diversity. New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 437–453 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d29428e992">24</a></sup>. Despite this separation, both lineages have maintained a similar genome size (~ 100 Mb) and neither has experienced a recent whole-genome duplication event, contrasting patterns observed in all other land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. science &#xA;                           332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d29428e996">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature &#xA;                           473, 97–100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d29428e999">25</a></sup>. <i>S. lepidophylla</i> and <i>S. moellendorffii</i> have maintained a surprising degree of collinearity with 36% (7849) of <i>S. moellendorffi</i> genes showing conserved order in <i>S. lepidophylla</i>. Genic regions are generally more compact in <i>S. lepidophylla</i>, correlating with the higher TE content (37.5%) in <i>S. moellendorffii</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">7</a>). By contrast, only ~ 15% of rice and core eudicot genes are collinear<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science &#xA;                           320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1025">26</a></sup>, reflecting the role of WGD events in shaping gene order and genome architecture. In the absence of WGD, any new traits and adaptations associated with duplicate genes in <i>Selaginella</i> must arise through local or segmental gene duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ohno, S. Evolution by Gene Duplication. (Springer-Verlag, 1970)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1032">27</a></sup>. <i>S. moellendorffii</i> has significantly more tandem gene duplications (TD) than <i>S. lepidophylla</i> with 6693 compared to 4170, but both share similar enrichment pattern (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">10</a>). TDs in <i>Selaginella</i> are enriched in molecular function GO terms related to kinase activity, ion and other small molecule binding among others (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">9</a>). Few TDs were annotated as transcription factors or highly connected metabolic genes, which is consistent with expectations under the gene balance hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Birchler, J. A. &amp; Veitia, R. A. The gene balance hypothesis: From classical genetics to modern genomics. Plant. Cell &#xA;                           19, 395–402, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.106.049338&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1059">28</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Bekaert, M., Edger, P. P., Pires, J. C. &amp; Conant, G. C. Two-phase resolution of polyploidy in the Arabidopsis metabolic network gives rise to relative and absolute dosage constraints. Plant. Cell &#xA;                           23, 1719–1728 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1062">29</a></sup>. Both <i>Selaginella</i> species have overlapping KEGG pathway enrichments across TDs including phenylpropanoid, cutin, and secondary metabolite metabolism (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">10</a>). <i>S. lepidophylla</i> has TDs enriched in phagosome pathways, which may be related to controlled autophagy during desiccation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Williams, B. et al. Trehalose accumulation triggers autophagy during plant desiccation. PLoS Genet. &#xA;                           11, e1005705 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1079">30</a></sup>.</p><p>Lycophytes are sister to all other vascular plants including angiosperms, the most diverse extant plant group on the planet. We surveyed changes in orthogroup composition across angiosperms and the two <i>Selaginella</i> species to identify patterns associated with WGD and life history traits. We identified 5891 orthogroups with representatives from every species and 69 orthogroups with expansion in angiosperms (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">11</a>). Angiosperm-dominated orthogroups have diverse GO terms and enriched KEGG pathways for alkaloid biosynthesis, proteolysis, and plant-pathogen interactions (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">13</a>). The angiosperm biased orthogroups are more highly connected than expected by chance (~ 2.7 edges per gene; <i>P</i> ≤ 10<sup>−20</sup>, Fisher’s exact test), fitting expectations for gene duplicates retained from WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Birchler, J. A. &amp; Veitia, R. A. The gene balance hypothesis: From classical genetics to modern genomics. Plant. Cell &#xA;                           19, 395–402, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.106.049338&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1104">28</a></sup>. The 55 Selaginella-biased orthogroups have GO enrichment in vesicle related processes and KEGG pathway enrichment in phenylpropanoid biosynthesis (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">15</a>). Most <i>Selaginella</i>-biased orthogroups are unique and completely uncharacterized, reflecting the need for more functional studies in other major plant lineages.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Origins and evolution of desiccation tolerance</h3><p>The transition from sea to land posed unique challenges for the ancestors of terrestrial plants. Early land plants needed to evolve protective mechanisms to survive periodic drying and low atmospheric water potential. Desiccation tolerance in angiosperms likely evolved from rewiring seed desiccation pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Costa, M. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants &#xA;                           3, 17038 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1125">2</a></sup>, but the origins of this trait in non-seed plants is unknown. We surveyed gene expression changes during the desiccation and rehydration process in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> and compared patterns to angiosperm resurrection plants. <i>S. lepidophylla</i> can survive for years or even decades in a desiccated state without loss in viability. Samples were taken from <i>S. lepidophylla</i> plants that were desiccated (0 h), 1, 6, and 24 h post rehydration (recovery), 120 h post rehydration (fully recovered), and after a 24 h dehydration (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig4">4a</a>). Despite prolonged quiescence, 77% of genes (21,062) have detectable expression in desiccated leaf tissue, implicating strong protective mechanisms to preserve the mRNA pool.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02546-5/MediaObjects/41467_2017_2546_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Genomic features of the rehydration and desiccation processes. <b>a</b> Overview of sampling for the rehydration and desiccation time course. Samples were taken from plants that were desiccated for 3 years (0), 1, 6, and 24 h post rehydration (recovery), 120 h post rehydration (fully recovered), and after a 24 h dehydration (de24 h). <b>b</b> Scaled transcript expression profiles (in transcripts per million, TPM) of representative gene co-expression clusters with decreased (10, 16, 34) and increased expression (5, 14, 34) during rehydration. <b>c</b> Heatmap of log2 transformed early light-induced protein (ELIP) expression with the two arrays of tandem duplicated genes in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> orthologous to the only ELIP in to <i>S. moellendorffii</i> highlighted on the left</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>We performed gene-co-expression analysis across the rehydration and subsequent dehydration RNAseq timecourse data. The 38 co-expression clusters fall under the following three general patterns with (1) high expression in desiccated tissues followed by decreases during rehydration, (2) increased expression during rehydration, and (3) steady expression across all samples (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig4">4b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">8</a>). Transcript levels are relatively consistent across the 0, 1, and 6 h post-rehydration time-points, with only 123 and 567 differentially expressed genes, respectively (0 h vs. 1 h and 1 h vs. 6 h). Clusters with increased expression during rehydration are enriched with functions related to DNA repair, oxidative and water stress response among others. Clusters with high expression in desiccated tissues and low expression during rehydration have functions related to cell protection and osmoprotectant accumulation as described in more detail below.</p><p>Gene duplications drive evolutionary innovation and tandem gene duplications (TDs) are important for adaptive evolution in dynamic environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Panchy, N., Lehti-Shiu, M. &amp; Shiu, S.-H. Evolution of gene duplication in plants. Plant Physiol. &#xA;                           171, 2294–2316 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1188">31</a></sup>. Several clusters with peak expression during desiccation are enriched in TDs, including many with previously characterized roles in desiccation tolerance (Cluster 5, fdr = 1.26 e<sup>−16</sup>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">9</a>). Early light-induced proteins (ELIPs) protect photosynthetic apparatus against photo-oxidative damage under high light and other abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hutin, C. et al. Early light-induced proteins protect Arabidopsis from photooxidative stress. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. &#xA;                           100, 4921–4926 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1197">32</a></sup>. Land plants typically have 1–2 ELIPs but <i>S. lepidophylla</i> has undergone a major expansion to 23 ELIPs. Most ELIPs are located in three tandem arrays with 8, 7, and 2 members, and the largest array is syntenic to the single ELIP found in <i>S. moellendorffii</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig4">4c</a>). ELIPs are universally highly expressed during desiccation and show co-expression with other desiccation-related genes including heat-shock proteins, DNA repair enzymes, oleosins, LEAs, and others (enriched in cluster 10, fdr = 6.70 e<sup>−23</sup>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#Fig3">3c</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">16</a>). A similar expansion of ELIPs was identified in the resurrection plant <i>Boea hygrometrica</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Xiao, L. et al. The resurrection genome of Boea hygrometrica: A blueprint for survival of dehydration. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. &#xA;                           112, 5833–5837 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1223">6</a></sup>, suggesting these proteins are likely involved in the convergent evolution of desiccation tolerance.</p><p>LEA proteins function in membrane stability and macromolecular protection with roles in seed development, response to abiotic stresses, and desiccation tolerance in angiosperm resurrection plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Costa, M. et al. A footprint of desiccation tolerance in the genome of Xerophyta viscosa. Nat. Plants &#xA;                           3, 17038 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1230">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gechev, T. S. et al. Molecular mechanisms of desiccation tolerance in the resurrection glacial relic Haberlea rhodopensis. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. &#xA;                           70, 689–709 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1233">33</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Challabathula, D. &amp; Bartels, D. Desiccation tolerance in resurrection plants: new insights from transcriptome, proteome and metabolome analysis. Front. Plant Sci. &#xA;                           4, 482 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1236">34</a></sup>. <i>S. lepidophylla</i> has an expansion in LEA proteins compared to <i>S. moellendorffii</i> with 65 vs 36 across six LEA subfamilies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">17</a>). The LEA2 and seed maturation protein (SMP) subfamilies show the most dramatic expansion with 31 and 11 members, respectively. LEAs are dispersed across the genome and few are found in tandem arrays. LEAs are among the most highly expressed transcripts in the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> desiccation/rehydration timecourse with 48 showing significant expression changes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">10</a>). Interestingly, LEAs are also highly expressed in well-hydrated tissues, suggesting they may be involved in constitutive protective mechanisms, similar to several osmoprotectants.</p><p>Desiccation-related genes are found in large tandem arrays in the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome with individual genes having similar expression patterns. This reflects the importance of TD in the evolution of novel traits and the advantages of gene proximity for coordinated expression. Patterns of gene expression during desiccation in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> mirror the pathways activated in angiosperm resurrection plants. These pathways were either independently co-opted through convergent evolution in both lineages or present in the common ancestor of early land plants. The latter explanation is more likely as dehydration tolerance was acquired during the colonization of land. This hypothesis can be further refined with additional non-seed resurrection plant genomes.</p><p>The extreme haplotype-specific variation in <i>Selaginella</i> has not been observed in other sequenced plant genomes. This dynamic flux of accumulating and purging sequences likely serves two purposes. Strong selective pressures for removal of excess LTR retrotransposons act to preserve proper gene and <i>cis</i>-element spacing, which in turn maintains the small genome size. Unequal crossing over between divergent haplotypes and LTR-mediated gene duplications create a reservoir of new genes to facilitate adaptive evolution. In this context, haplotype-specific gene content may supplement the diversity lost in the absence of WGD.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">DNA extraction and sequencing</h3><p>The <i>Selaginella lepidophylla</i> plants used in this study were collected in the Chihuahuan Desert in the Southwestern US. These plants were confirmed to be <i>S. lepidophylla</i> based on sequence homology to published vouchered specimens with the following nucleotide identities based on BLAST: rbcL (99.6%; GenBank: AF419051.1) ITS2 (99.8%; GenBank: AF419002.1) 28S (99.8%; GenBank: AJ507594.1). Desiccated plants were stored at 20 °C in dark conditions for roughly 3 years prior to this experiment. Rehydrated plants were maintained in growth chambers under 24 °C day, 20 °C night, 12-h photoperiod, and 200 μmol m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> light. High-molecular-weight genomic DNA was extracted from young leaf tissue of a single <i>S. lepidophylla</i> plant using a modified nuclei preparation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Zhang, H. B., Zhao, X., Ding, X., Paterson, A. H. &amp; Wing, R. A. Preparation of megabase‐size DNA from plant nuclei.  Plant J. &#xA;                           7, 175–184 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1302">35</a></sup> followed by phenol chloroform purification to remove residual contaminants. DNA quality was verified on a low-concentration agarose gel (0.5% W/V) prior to library construction. gDNA was sheared to a 30 kb target size followed by end repair and adapter ligation. The final library was size selected using a BluePippen size selection system (Sage Science) followed by purification using AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter). High-molecular-weight libraries were sequenced using the PacBio RsII instrument with P6C4 chemistry. A total of 21 SMRT cells were sequenced. A PCR-free Illumina DNAseq library was constructed using the same high-molecular weight DNA following the manufactories instructions. Paired end 300 bp reads were sequenced on an Illumina MiSeq platform yielding a total of 80× Illumina sequence coverage for error correction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">RNA extraction and sequencing</h3><p>
                           <i>S. lepidophylla</i> plants were maintained in growth chambers under 16-h-light/8-h-night regime at 22 ± 2 °C for desiccation/rehydration experiments. Sampling at the 0 h (desiccated), 24 and 120 h post rehydration and 24 h post dehydration were taken at <i>T</i> = 0 to avoid any variance associated with natural circadian oscillation. Sampling was done in triplicate with three biological replicates sequenced for each timepoint. The relative water content (RWC) for the <i>S. lepidophylla</i> rehydration time-course was calculated using the following equation: RWC = [(FW − DW)/(SW − DW)], where FW, DW, and SW indicate fresh weight, dry weight, and saturated weight, respectively. DW was obtained after drying plant leaf tissue at 103 °C for 24 h and SW was obtained after submerging the leaf tissue in water for 36 h. RWC for each sample can be found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#MOESM1">18</a>. Total RNA was extracted from 100 mg of ground <i>S. lepidophylla</i> leaf tissues using Omega-biotek E.Z.N.A. Plant RNA Kit (Omega-biotek), according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Two micrograms of total RNA was used for construction Illumina TruSeq stranded mRNA libraries following the manufactures protocol (Illumina). Multiplexed pooled libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq4000 under paired-end 150 nt mode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">DNA extraction and methylation sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA for methylation profiling was extracted from 500 mg of ground tissues using the CTAB method, followed by RNase digestion and column purification using Zymo Research genomic DNA clean &amp; concentrator™ (Zymo Research). In total, 500 ng of genomic DNA was first sheared to an average size of 400–800 bp using Covaris M200. Libraries were constructed using KAPA Hyper Prep Kits (KapaBiosystem, #KK8504) according to the manufacturer’s protocol. The sheared DNA fragments were end-repaired and A-tailed followed by adapter ligation. Adapter-ligated fragments were subjected to bisulfite conversion using EZ DNA Methylation-Lightning™ Kit (Zymo Research). Bisulfite-converted DNA was amplified using KAPA HiFi HotStart Uracil + Ready Mix (KapaBiosystem) with 8 PCR cycles. PCR products were then size-selected by gel electrophoresis for the range of 300–600 bp and recovered by Zymoclean<sup>®</sup> Gel DNA Recovery Kit (Zymo Research). Multiplexed pooled libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq4000 platform under paired-end 150 nt mode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Genome assembly</h3><p>
                           <i>S. lepidophylla</i> PacBio reads collectively span 19.3 Gb representing 175× genome coverage with a filtered subread N50 length of 18 kb. Raw reads were error corrected and assembled using the Canu (V1.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1350">13</a></sup> assembler with the following parameters: minReadLength = 3000, GenomeSize = 110 Mb, and minOverlapLength = 2000. Other Canu parameters were left as default. The Falcon assembler (V0.3.0) was also tested but the overall contiguity was much lower (N50–110 kb) and a higher proportion of haplotypes were collapsed. Contigs were polished using a reiterative approach with two rounds of Quiver (V2.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods &#xA;                           10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1354">14</a></sup> with a minimum subread length = 5000 bp, minimum polymerase read quality = 0.8, maximum divergence percentage = 30 and minimum anchor size = 15. The polished contigs underwent a final round of error correction using high-coverage 300 bp PE Illumina data using Pilon (V1.21)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE &#xA;                           9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1358">15</a></sup>. Quality trimmed Illumina reads were aligned to the Quiver polished contigs using bowtie2 (V2.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods &#xA;                           9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1362">36</a></sup> with default parameters. Reads were locally re-aligned around insertions/deletions (indels) using the IndelRealigner from the genome analysis tool kit (GATK; V3.7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. &#xA;                           20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1367">37</a></sup>. Pilon parameters were as follows: --flank 7, --K 51, and --mindepth 30. Illumina reads were re-aligned to the final polished genome to verify no residual errors remained using GATK.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Identification of haplotype-specific regions</h3><p>Assembled haplotypes were identified using a two-step approach based on gene level microsynteny followed by whole-genome alignment. Gene synteny avoided spurious hits and misclassification around repetitive elements with whole-genome alignment facilitating base-pair resolution of overlapping haplotype regions. Syntenic gene pairs were identified with BLAST followed by filtering for only collinear pairs using the MCSCAN toolkit (V1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           40, e49–e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1380">38</a></sup>. This approach flagged 11,847 duplicated gene pairs collectively representing ~45% of genes across 28 Mb of the genome. The exact boundaries of haplotype overlaps and haplotype-specific structural variants were identified by whole-genome alignment using MUMmer (V3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1384">39</a></sup>. The unmasked genome was aligned against itself using NUCmer with default parameters and a minimum overlap size of 2 kb. Only hits in contigs with collinear gene pairs were considered and overlaps in these regions were manually filtered removing misalignments in repetitive regions. In total, 33.7 Mb of assembled haplotype sequences were identified including 105 high-confidence haplotype-specific insertions/deletions (indel). The boundaries of each indel were manually refined using the GEvo module in CoGe (<a href="https://genomevolution.org">https://genomevolution.org</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences.  Plant J. &#xA;                           53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1395">40</a></sup>. Haplotype-specific indels were verified through alignment of raw PacBio reads spanning the boundary regions in both haplotypes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Transposable element identification</h3><p>Non-autonomous DNA transposons in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> were identified by MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1410">41</a></sup> and were classified according to the sequence of termini and length of target site duplication (TSD) provided in the output from MITE-Hunter based on the guide of Zhao et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhao, D., Ferguson, A. A. &amp; Jiang, N. What makes up plant genomes: The vanishing line between transposable elements and genes. Biochim. Biophys. Acta &#xA;                           1859, 366–380 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1414">42</a></sup>. Candidate long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs) in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> were identified using LTR_Finder (V1.02)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1421">43</a></sup> and LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics &#xA;                           9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1426">44</a></sup>. LTR_retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal-repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. DOI: https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.17.01310 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1430">45</a></sup> was used to filter out false LTR retrotransposons using structural and sequence features of target site duplications, terminal motifs, and LTR-RT Pfam domains. Whole-genome annotation of LTR retrotransposons was done by RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Tarailo‐Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 4.10. 11-14.10. 14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1441">46</a></sup> using the non-redundant LTR-RT library constructed by LTR_retriever. DNA transposons that were nest-inserted in LTR retrotransposons were identified and removed in the LTR library using a Perl scrip “Purger.pl” from the LTR_retriever package. Non-LTR retrotransposons and other interspersed repeats were identified using RepeatModeler (V1.0.8) (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>). While RepeatModeler also reports LTR retrotransposons, their sequences were examined using TE-specific Pfam domains by HMMER (V3.1b2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. &#xA;                           7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1452">47</a></sup>. Among 34 RepeatModeler-predicted LTR retrotransposons, none of them were exclusively matched with LTR-specific Pfam domains, thus all these sequences were labeled as unknown repeats instead of LTR retrotransposons. Candidate tandem repeats were identified by Tandem Repeat Finder (trf409.linux64)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1457">48</a></sup> and were masked by the TEs identified in previous steps. Unmasked candidates were labeled as tandem repeats, and the sequence redundancy were reduced using Cd-hit (V4.6.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           22, 1658–1659 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1461">49</a></sup> with parameters -c 0.8, -G 0.8, -s 0.9, -aL 0.9, -aS 0.9, -M 0, and -T 5. The Perl script “Purger.pl” from LTR_retriever and the plant protein database “alluniRefprexp082813” from MAKER (V2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics &#xA;                           12, 1–14 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1465">50</a></sup> were used to remove any protein-coding sequences that were accidentally identified in these TEs. The top 10 most abundant repeat families (based on genome mass) were manually curated through alignment of multiple family members so to determine their boundary, termini, and TSD, which were used to classify the relevant repeat.</p><p>Solo LTRs were identified from the whole-genome annotation of LTR-RT using the Perl script “solo_finder.pl” in the LTR_retriever package. In brief, independent LTR sequences (no other LTR-related sequence presented in 300 bp distance) that cover at least 80% of the library LTR entry with alignment score &gt;300 and at least 100 bp in length were identified as solo LTR. The solo-intact LTR ratio was calculated based on the number of solo LTR over the number of intact LTR-RT for each family, which was estimated using the Perl script “solo_intact_ratio.pl” in the LTR_retriever package. Since LTR length is positively correlated with the formation of solo LTRs, we only compared the S/I ratio using elements with comparable LTR length. This was achieved by using the 95 percentile of LTR length in <i>S. lepidophylla</i> (90–1540 bp) to screen for LTR-RT families within into this range in the rice and Arabidopsis genomes.</p><p>Estimation of insertion time for each intact LTR element was provided by LTR_retriever using the formula of <i>T</i> = <i>K</i>/2<i>μ</i>, where <i>K</i> is the divergence rate approximated by percent identity and adjusted using the Jukes-Cantor model for non-coding sequences. The neutral mutation rate of <i>μ</i> = 1.3 × 10<sup>–8</sup> mutations per bp per year was used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA &#xA;                           101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1496">51</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Genome annotation</h3><p>The <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome was annotated using MAKER (V 2.31.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. &#xA;                           18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1511">52</a></sup>. A set of high-quality transcripts for gene prediction were assembled with StringTie (V1.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. &#xA;                           33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1515">53</a></sup> using the stranded, paired end Illumina RNAseq data described above. Quality filtered RNAseq reads were aligned to the unmasked <i>S. lepidophylla</i> genome using TopHat (V2.1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-02546-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d29428e1522">54</a></sup> and the accepted_hits.bam file was used as input for StringTie.
